And it doesn't matter whether you like Hip-Hop, Punk, R&B, Jazz, Heavy Metal, Pop, Glee Covers, and everything else in between, because this CD is so good that you will enjoy listening to at least parts of it. It has good melodies, deep lyrics, virtually everything and anything you could want from one album.<br /><br />This is that record.<br /><br />\\"Ceremonials\\" by Florence + the Machine is the sophomore-slump-defying work by the welsh band, released in 2011. I honestly cannot reccomend this enough. Each and every song is absolutely amazing in its own right, deserving and worthy of multiple listens, well-provoked thought, and even a few music video searches here and there.<br /><br />Florence + the Machine is one of those rare bands that really has the whole package.<br /><br />I'll get to some individual tracks in a second, but, overall the sound of the band is a modern rock archetype, with a few twists. The first is the lead singer Florence, the red headed muse who literally has the voice of an angel. And she shows it off on this album with many vocal acrobatics and vowel holds. The next twist is the instruments that the band uses. You have the standard guitars, drums, backup vocals, keyboards, etc. But then you have harps, organs, maybe a violin here and there, bongo drums.<br /><br />It is a melding of the old and the new. And the final product is mystifying.<br /><br />The CD starts of strong with \\" Only if for a Night\\" (lyrics in my title), which is ultra-classy. Of course, right afterword is \\"Shake it Out\\" the first single off the record. Purely mystifying. Then there is \\"What the Water Gave Me\\", also powerful.<br /><br />THEN, the mood starts to change a bit. (Although I occasionally still listen to Lover to Lover.)<br /><br />Things instantly pick back up with \\"No Light, No Light\\" which is perhaps my favorite track on the CD, maybe even in the bands collective work. Organ, HArp, Drums, Guitars, I love it all.<br /><br />Then, unfortunately, you get *the* weakest track of the album \\"Seven Devils.\\" Among such happy songs, or at least sad songs with happy figurative language, Seven Devils just strikes me as cold, slow, and depressing. But it's still good for a few listens. That's how good this band is!<br /><br />\\"Heartlines\\" and \\"Spectrum\\" are next, which actually both tie for my second favorite song. They are both super-upbeat, ultra-realistic, and just masterful in execution. I listen to Heartlines while driving home, late at night, always with a huge smile on my face.<br /><br />Finally, you get \\"All This and Heaven Too\\" and \\"Leave my Body\\", more of the same, but still super strong.<br /><br />And that's a prevailing theme, a lot of the songs on here have the same thematic sound, but they are all different enough that they blend together and symphonize with each other, without sounding repetitive.<br /><br />Then there are the Bonus Tracks, which, to be honest, are kind of hit or miss. But all still decent enough to warrant a buy if you're interested.<br /><br />\\"Remain Nameless\\" is okay, and \\"Bedroom Hymns\\" is only alright. The Demo version of \\"What the Water Gave Me\\" is worth a listen, also, because it's interesting to see how the band evolved and developed. The best bonus track, in my opinion, is \\"Strangeness and Charm\\", which I wish made it onto the complete album *instead* of \\"Seven Devils\\", but that's just me. Both are outstanding songs.<br /><br />In conclusion, if you're still usnure about buying this album, download \\"Shake it Out.\\" If you like it, check out \\"No Light, No Light\\", \\" Spectrum\\", and \\"Heartlines\\". 1400052181|5.0|The Immortal Life of Henrietta Lacks by Rebecca Skloot is a very unique book. It tells the true story of the poor, African American woman named Henrietta Lacks who allowed for some of the biggest advances in Science in recent memory. Then, it continues to chronicle the hardships the family has since gone through, including personal interviews with the author herself.<br /><br />Oh, yeah, and it totally reads like a novel.<br /><br />Ms. Skloot writes in such powerful, poingant prose that does something I haven't seen since the likes of Truman Capote ([[ASIN:0375507906 In Cold Blood]]) or Harper Lee ([[ASIN:0061743526 To Kill a Mockingbird: 50th Anniversary Edition]] ... did I really have to tell you that one?), who both pioneered a genre they called \\"creative non-fiction.\\" In my opinion, this book is a clear descendant of that mindset, and the overall effect is beautiful.<br /><br />The book is divided into four parts, which, basically are: Henrietta's life, The Scientists who dealt with her, Rebecca Skloot's mission, and Skloot's work with the family. Then there is a conclusion that wraps things up nicely.<br /><br />What you read in this book will enrage you, as all great books do, but what you should know going into this is: proceeds from the book are going to help the Lacks family.<br /><br />See, writing a review for a non-fiction book is hard, especially a good one, because there aren't any dry parts I can complain about or plot lines I could discuss. Although this book has a plot, I would feel terrible to spoil anything at all for anyone. So I'm not going to.<br /><br />Just let me say that the story in this book almost made me cry. Oh, yes. Did I mention I'm 17 years old and a guy? And it almost moved me to tears. I cannot stress how powerful this book is. Go, read it. Introduce it to your book club. Gain some knowledge of the world around you.<br /><br />And embark on the journey that Skloot will lead for you.

0345803485|1.0|Fifty Shades of Grey is not the first book I've thrown across the room - it's the second - but it is the first book I also kicked after it hit the floor.<br /><br />Am I a book snob? Sure. I have a degree in English, I write, and I've taught literature in public schools. But I am also the all-American mother and housewife who can enjoy my small share of erotic fiction without judging the literary chops of the authors who pander to me.<br /><br />However. \\"E.L. James,\\" for whatever she set out to accomplish, should have first accomplished finding either a proofreader or an editor at the esteemed publishing houses who bothered to put her trilogy in print.<br /><br />Let me say that everything I am about to complain about is based on nothing but the first 40-something pages of book #1, because I got no further.<br /><br />First, the Fifty Shades of Grey is set around a college campus in Washington state. That's America, y'all. So the charming British-isms that James thinks make her sound like a high-minded writer (living in London though she is) are incongruities. Eighteen-year-old American co-eds do not \\"phone\\" their roommates - they \\"call\\" them. They should be making \\"dinner,\\" not \\"supper,\\" and they certainly should not be ordering their tea \\"bag out.\\"<br /><br />(In addition to being a reader, writer, and teacher, I also paid my dues as a waitress, and I never once served a guest, even a guest with a polished overseas accent, tea with the \\"bag out.\\" The phrase just does not exist in our culture. And the only people who referred to their meal as \\"supper\\" were over sixty and southern.)<br /><br />Second, on page 24 to be specific, the word \\"besieged\\" is used twice within a few sentences of each other, describing the same crowd of consumers at a hardware store. I already suggested introducing James to a proofreader - now I shall suggest a thesaurus.<br /><br />And ditto with my third complaint. Her characters can only \\"mutter\\" and \\"murmur\\" so many times on a page, before we, the readers, might desperately offer to buy the author a new verb. Regardless of the overuse of \\"mutter,\\" specifically, I would venture to suggest that it's the wrong verb altogether. The lovely and shrinking Anastasia would certainly not be \\"muttering.\\" She might be speaking haltingly, or she might be whispering shyly, or she might be choking out an awkward response. But \\"muttering\\"? Let's use a dictionary, in addition to our thesaurus, James.<br /><br />And make sure it's an American dictionary, not a British one.<br /><br />Fourth. I cut James a break when Anastasia and Christian show no continuity in whether they refer to each other by their first names, or as Ms. Steele and Mr. Grey. (Aren't those clever last names? What were the chances?) I decided the characters were on such unstable, sexually-charged ground with each other that whether to be formal or familiar was a question constantly in a state of flux. But when the two inhumanly beautiful characters sit at a coffee shop (while the American teenaged Ana drinks her tea \\"bag out\\") and have a conversation regarding why Christian does not invite Anastasia to call him by his first name - without ever mentioning the fact that she already has, repeatedly - that's when Fifty Shades of Grey became airborne.<br /><br />Housewives of America, or England, with a penchant for smut - please, put pen to paper and continue to delight and thrill us, we with the small boring lives, who would love to invite a Christian Grey into our beds. But before your book sees a binding, please also make sure that you're not bringing the entire professions of writing and publishing down into the steamy lower-levels of erotic literature with you. I understand that I needed to lower my expectations when I read on the publisher's page that this book originally appeared online as a serialized novel, written by \\"Snowqueen Icedragon,\\" but even a Snowqueen could have used an editor.

0374275637|3.0|Let me start by declaring a bias: I love pop-science books. I'm fairly happy with the hard core scientific tomes too, but pop-science brings together two of my passions - science and literature, and hence I tend to view most such books through a favourable lens.<br /><br />Fast & Slow started off fairly well, and Kahneman's writing felt lucid and thoughtful. The introductory chapters held promise of a book that would develop well and give me lots to think about, and provide some useful guidance in my everyday life. About halfway through the book, however, against my strong desire to go on, I gave up, and here's why: the book was going nowhere. It wasn't that it stopped being insightful - it was just that it seemed more like a collection of interesting examples than a collected, coherent development. Individually, the chapters did not feel unpleasant, and in fact contained a lot of knowledge and insight that I was unfamiliar with and found engrossing, but my objection is to putting all these chapters together as a book. Most books, outside of the text/ reference book variety, effectively become part of the greater body of literature, and should be viewed through that lens. I don't believe that books can be measured by different yardsticks on account of being clever, or being written by somebody famous or important, and consequently, I believe this book falls short.<br /><br />So, despite my bias towards this genre, my recommendation would be to read it as a background book, or better still, as a series of articles over a long-ish period of time. You might save yourself some of the disappointment that way and be able to enjoy, and complete this book.

B006QVRUS2|4.0|There's an old SNL skit starring Will Ferrell that depicted a TV newscast team as it lost its teleprompter, and the ensuing bloodbath that eventually occurred.  The term \\"bloodbath\\" was correctly used, as the lack of prepared text somehow paves the way for the news team to devolve into its primal state, and by the end of ten minutes they actually begin cannibalizing one another in the midst of its hyperbolic chaos.<br /><br />The movie \\"Carnage\\" will probably impress you in much the same way.  Essentially a long one-act play, it enacts the meeting of two relatively affluent married couples who have previously agreed to get together in the living room of one of their apartments to discuss a previous spat involving their eleven year old sons on an urban playground, wherein one boy struck the other in the face with a stick.<br /><br />You might expect that that thread of a story is perhaps not enough to base a movie upon, especially given the fact that there is almost no change of setting other than a brief bathroom scene.  But you'd be wrong.  In fact, I was surprised when it ended a mere 75 minutes later.<br /><br />First off, \\"Carnage\\" is hilarious.  And what pedigree: directed by Roman Polanski, its cast consists entirely of Christoph Waltz, Kate Winslet, John C. Reilly, and Jodie Foster, other than a couple of long distance shots of some important secondary characters.<br /><br />Equally important is this statement: be aware this is Theater of the Uncomfortable.  If you are not a fan of, say, a long elevator ride with an arguing couple, you might not be able to sit through this film.  But if you can stick it out you will be richly rewarded.<br /><br />The movie begins with the wrapping up of a typed statement by the parents of the injured boy, with only one word substitution suggested by the parents of the boy who struck the other: originally said to be \\"armed\\" with a stick, it is later changed to \\"carrying.\\"  That said, Penelope (Foster) prints out the letter, the two couples separate to head out to an elevator as her husband Michael (played by Reilly) innocently suggests they might like to stay for an espresso.  With a nod, Alan and Nancy (Weisz and Winslet), step back into the apartment.<br /><br />And so it begins...<br /><br />Please take me at my word: this movie gets very uncomfortable.  As each couple absorbs or makes little digs and innuendos, it initially appears that the battle lines will be clear: it's the Cowans vs. the Longstreets, and when it all eventually breaks down it is natural to assume each parent will vigorously defend their son.<br /><br />At first terribly polite, the couples' marital issues are egged on by incessant phone calls and dog barks and alcohol and, well, vomit, forcing bothersome traits and affectionate nicknames and paranoias and prejudices to surface in all their ugliness.<br /><br />Yet there's much, much more, and I think it is fair to say that no one survives this debacle.  Not that we will witness the outcome; no, as I mentioned before, the film ends abruptly, but by then the battle lines have become so blurred and far-ranging that it is fair to say that the boys are the least of the offenders.  To paraphrase an old expression: no one gets out of here undamaged.<br /><br />I don't want to say too much...the beauty of this extremely talented cast is that they are able to ante up little moments and idiosyncrasies until they become cannon-fire, and there is no one here that I can truly admire when it's all over. The first two notes of the song require that the vocalist travel upwards an entire octave (a sizable yet exacting leap from the first to the 2nd note).  In jazz it's long been an unwritten \\"rule\\" that even the best improvisers (Louis, Miles, Coltrane; Ella, Sarah, Roberta Gambarini) respect the composer's original melody for the first and last choruses (by contrast, everything that happens in between the \\"in\\" and \\"out\\" choruses depends upon the improvisatory inventiveness of the performer). B0071XO8RA|5.0|This appears to be my first(oops, its my second one...) review on a book by a digital age writer - you know, those people who are publishing their story without a dedicated publisher's support (or approval).<br /><br />So, how to begin? First of all - there are five stories in there; and to my pleasant surprise all are somewhere between good and very good. One of them is absolutely mind-blowing, the best story I have read since 1982, and since that time I've read more than 1500 novels and countless short stories. So, trust me on that one: this collection is not only well worth the money and a good read but it also contains one true pinnacle of science fiction story-telling of the decade. And no, I won't tell which is it, so get it, read for yourself, judge for yourself.<br /><br />Hugh Howey's writing is excellent, he manages to get you hooked instantly into the story while leaving yourself enough room to make yourself at home in his stories until you are completely immersed in a background setting nobody in his right mind would really want to. It is, all things considered, counted and labeled, a dystopian world's image he is painting inside your skull. And he keeps surprising you, often you think 'OK, now I have figured it out.' until you realize you haven't. And heres the master at work: everytime you experiece such a surprise you have essentially fooled yourself somewhere along the story - Hugh never did hide anything in this story, but your mind hardly ever connects all the dots. 0061969656|4.0|This is the least interesting of the series so far.  It has all of the elements that you would expect but it doesn't move the story forward in any significant way.  The previous books really stand alone as novels; this one reads more like an episode.<br /><br />I think I can guess the reason for this.  When Cornwell wrote the Richard Sharpe series, he placed his protagonist at all of the pivotal moments of the Penninsular War but he arrived at Waterloo with no more war and a public that wanted more Sharpe books.  Like Forester's Hornblower series, he was able to go back and write about the younger Sharpe, but then he had to shoe-horn stories into the gaps between the earlier books.  My guess is that he swore never to put himself through that again and so the Saxon stories are going to plod along in strict chronological order rather than skip ahead to the more significant bits.<br /><br />Like all of this series, writing in the first person seems to tempt Cornwell to channel a little more of his own anti-Christian childhood feelings through the character which hurts the historical accuracy a bit.  But the careful re-creation of the political, military, and material conditions in Britain at the end of the ninth century, combined with really good storytelling make up for it.

B0002XMF5S|2.0|For anyone not familiar with the story of this records inception, I will give a quick recap to bring you up to speed.  This album was intended to be a solo album by Tony Iommi, however the record label insisted that the Black Sabbath name be used.  When viewed in that scope, clearly Tony had no intention of carrying over Black Sabbath sound in any capacity, nor was it his intent to sell records with the name recognition.  So let's divorce ourselves from the bias of the Black Sabbath cannon and attempt to understand what this album has to offer.<br /><br />\\"Seventh Star\\" is more of an attempt touch base on what you might consider the blues rock roots of Tony Iommi.  As for the vocals, enter Glenn Hughes, possibly the most \\"soulful\\" singer Iommi has worked with.  A man who was no stranger to the vision Iommi had on this album.  The real question I have to ask is this - Why isn't this album better?  The ingredients seem to be here, some polished blues riffs, dirty sounding bluesy leads, and a passionate frontman.  Sounds like I have done nothing but make an argument FOR this album.  Let's start with the positives.  The title track \\"Seventh Star\\" has attained legendary status for me.  Whether viewed solely on it's own merits, or within the larger scope of Iommi's work as a whole, it stands as one of my all time favorite cuts in and outside of Black Sabbath.  \\"Heart Like A Wheel\\" is a blues rock dirge, with some tasty guitar leads.  It has a sinister plodding feel, that is, sinister in a bluesy, hard rock way.  Even the upbeat bouncing \\"Turn To Stone\\" deserves a spin, despite it's somewhat cheesy nature.  And now THERE is the segue ladies and gentleman.  The cheese on this album is too much for me personally to take.  Just listen to the 1-2 punch \\"In For the Kill\\" and \\"No Stranger to Love\\".  Almost wrecks my ability to listen to the album.  How about \\"Danger Zone\\", a song that sounds like it's riff was stolen from Poison or Motley Crue.  I'd say listen to this album for yourself and find your own hidden gems, I found mine.  However, I can't heap praises onto something that I myself only have found a small handful of songs to enjoy.  Two Stars.  Buy it and find out for yourself.yourself and find your own hidden gems, I found mine. 1594202710|4.0|As a Chinese, the name \\"Kissigner\\" has been familiar to me ever since high school years. He has long been labeled as \\"the old friend of China\\". So when this name is associated with a book titled \\"On China\\". I assumed it to be extraordinary and couldn't wait to order it. It seems I did the right thing as I constantly found myself so focused when reading this book on commuter train that I missed my stop.<br /><br />I found this in one of the previous comments; \\"When you read a book like this, you will have a better understanding of China than 98% of the people living in China and 95% of the Chinese people living in America.\\" That's probably too exaggerating a statement that Kissinger himself would be reluctant to make. A more realistic way to put it would be \\"If you read this book, you will have a better understanding of China than 95% of the people living in America\\" I think that's closer to Kissinger's goal for writing this book - to facilitate a better understanding of China as a large, old and singular country, the history and development of Sino-US relation, and its possible directions in the next decades. By reading this book and some of the readers' comments, I'd say that purpose has been successfully fulfilled.<br /><br />Chapter 1 -3  History<br /><br />No resource on China could be deemed completed without exploring its history, especially in the past 200 years. The first three chapters is an attempt in that regard, Dr. Kissinger took his time to fashion a somewhat ambitious but at times superficial analysis of China's 4000-year history. Here he makes a few points that will be repeatedly referenced in later chapters, for example, the application of Sun Tzu's \\"Art of War\\" in Mao's strategy, and Chinese leaders' aloofness and self-reliance represented in the emperor's dealing with the Macartney envoy in 1790s, and later found in Mao's and Deng's negotiations with the Americans. Kissinger does a satisfactory job here in helping western readers form a historic view of China. The only thing missing is probably the period from the fall of Qing dynasty in 1912 to the founding of the People's Republic in 1949, which had a deep impact on the historical view and emotional feeling of Chinese, but be treated by the author with merely a few paragraphs. Even though the book is \\"On China\\", it's actually on US-China relation. The 1912-1949 period is probably not that significant in setting the stage.<br /><br />Chapter 4-11 The Mao era<br /><br />One of the Amazon comments says \\"Kissinger is in awe of Mao Zedong\\" so his analysis of Mao is intrinsically biased.  I'm not sure if that is true, but Kinsinger's assessment of Mao is surprisingly close to that of many contemporary Chinese (including me), whether they have personally experienced the cultural revolution or not. After all, this is the one that made China united again after decades of weakness, shame and torture. If you understand how important the founding of Israel is to Jews, you'll understand how important the founding of the People's Republic is to Chinese. In China we say \\"Mao made Chinese stand up, and Deng made Chinese stand rich\\". It is likely that during the process of US-China rapprochement, it was crucial for Kissinger as the key player to appreciate the Chinese interlocutors' decision making process. Even though Kissinger does not endorse the continuous revolution that Mao promoted, he might have moved closer to a Mao-sympathizer when building personal relationships with Mao and Zhou. Particularly, the authors writes, \\"In some sixty years of public life, I have encountered no more compelling figure than Zhou Enlai\\". This seems to be in line with the vprevelant Chinese view that Zhou has been the most respected premier and diplomat, probably the most respected politician in the history of the People's Republic of China at all, even more so than Mao.<br /><br />No wonder that the author devoted about half of the book to this topic as personal admiration was compoundedby the significance of Mao's decision in recreating the landscape of Sino-US relation and even the balance of power in cold war. Many of the events described are not new to people who grew up in China. They may be revealing to American readers. What I found instructive is the author's justification of American's entry into the Korean War as well as it's commitment to defending Taiwan. In communist propaganda both were characterized as imperial intrusion, which led to direct military confrontation between China and the US. Now that I read these chapters, I started to understand that the American decisions were somewhat based on America's perception of the practice of democracy and its policy to contain communist expansion in Asia. As the author put it - both sides are right.<br /><br />I agree with the view that using the traditional Weiqi game (originated in China) to explain national strategy is a great simplification, which the author seems to enjoy doing. If sending American troops to Korea and sending the 7th Fleet to the Taiwan Strait were both Weiqi stones to contain China, then what about Soviet Union deploying misses in Cuba? This is not really about Weiqi.

0061726834|5.0|For I while I've wondered about why so many people loved Delirium. It didn't particularly appeal to me and the description never sounded that interesting. I finally caved and had to see for myself what exactly everyone was talking about. Shortly into the book, I understood. It's not the quite the story that's amazing but it's the writing. It's beautiful and so remarkably well crafted that I can hear English teachers and librarians across the country giving thanks to Lauren Oliver for writing a young adult novel of this caliber. I seriously think the writing in this novel should be studied.<br /><br />In a nutshell, love is illegal in Oliver's dystopian society.  Love, also known as delirium, is considered a disease and all citizens must undergo surgery to cure them for the delirium by the age of 18. Lena, the main character, is almost 18 and is looking forward to the day she is cured.  The argument against love is that it is the cause of many of the worlds problems. \\"It kills you both when you have it and when you don't.\\" Rebels are imprisoned, killed, and beaten by the enforcers of the law. It's hard to imagine our society ever banning love but Oliver makes her world is believable and surprisingly realistic. Even as I describe this book I don't feel like I'm doing it much justice.  I assure you there is more to Delirium than what I've described.<br /><br />One of the things I enjoyed about Delirium was the way the author describes love.  Yes it is a book about love, but Oliver clearly paints the positives and negative sides. She describes both sides so well that you root for the rebels and understand why love is banned and law is so forcefully upheld.  For most of the book, Lena, is just discovering love. She is happy when she is with Alex, constantly smiling and laughing; and when she is away she feels the pain of missing him.  Their secret love keeps them living in fear of being discovered. People who have been cured seem to be without a soul and the society Lena once found comfort in, becomes a future she cannot bear without Alex.<br /><br />Overall, I finally see what everyone is talking about it. B000WGWQG8|5.0|I have to say when I saw first saw the trailer for the HBO series, \\"John Adams\\" starring Paul Giamatti in the title role, I had some serious doubts as to whether or not I even wanted to see it. As an actor, Giamatti seemed to leave much to be desired if anything could be inferred by some of the minor parts he played in some incredibly abysmal examples of movies, such as: Howard Stern's Private Parts, My Best Friend's Wedding, and Big Momma's House.<br /><br />But in spite of his subpar resume, it turns out Giamatti CAN act. Giamatti creates an Adams who is as filled with self-doubt as he is patriotic fervor and the result is a believable, palpably human founding father who was as ambitious as he was flawed. The series begins on the evening of the Boston Massacre in 1770 and ends with the death of Adams in 1826- a span of some 56 years--which in and of itself is no small acting challenge but one that Giamatti manages to pull off rather convincingly.<br /><br />Laura Linney plays the forward-thinking, Abigail Adams, who was the intellectual equal and shrewd advisor of her famous husband. Linney is wonderful in the role. She readily conveys a character who is warm and God-fearing, yet possessed by unwavering Yankee pragmatism with a moral compass that seemingly never loses sight of its own personal North Star. Linney's effortless performance is a pleasure to watch and she easily captivates the viewer in much the same way that Abigail Adams charms the French naval officers, George Washington, Benjamin Rush and Thomas Jefferson.<br /><br />The series is superbly adapted from David McCullough's Pulitzer Prize-winning biography by the same title. Tom Hooper directs the series and does an exceptional job of it.  Unlike the heavy-handed direction that often typifies modern films, Hooper adroitly trusts the viewer to draw his/her own conclusions about various scenes or contentious situations, such as the frivolous French court's tepid reception of the bellicose, impatient Adams who is severely out of place as the American ambassador to France.<br /><br />Stephen Dillane is extremely well cast as Thomas Jefferson, who is as elegant-sounding as he is to look at; Tom Wilkinson plays a transparently self-promoting Benjamin Franklin who is seemingly unwilling to back American independence, or any patriotic cause, unless there is something in it for him; and Sarah Polley gives a poignant performance as the brave Nabby Adams who endues an unhappy marriage and then dies young, after undergoing a mastectomy with a minimal amount of laudanum as anesthetic.<br /><br />History buffs will enjoy the series which deftly combines historic fact with flesh and blood characters that are multi-faceted and compelling to watch.  Hooper's intimate direction easily draws the viewer into the respective scenes and helps the audience understand that the early days of American history were, in many ways, not much different than the stormy political climate in which we currently live in the present day; except that our founding fathers cared deeply about democracy and our current politicians do not.

Many of the reviewers prior to me have pointed some out but some that stuck in my craw were:<br /><br />Dogs behave heroically by accident or by random chance: I disagree wholeheartedly. When I laid down in the surf next to him, he grabbed me around the upper-arm with his teeth and firmly pulled me toward the shore. This wasn't a random act of protecting his food source. He thought he was protecting me. Another example would be when I was a child. I had wondered from home and our German Sheppard followed me. While I was only about a block from home, the German Sheppard would not let anyone near me until my parents came. Again, this wasn't a random heroic act. This was protection as he saw it.<br /><br />Dogs don't know right from wrong other than by human interaction: Again using personal experience, we currently have two dogs. When something happens in the house while we are at work (poop, pee, ripping up something they shouldn't), only one of our dogs greets us at the door. The other was hiding. Obviously when I first open the door, I don't know a thing. Another example was again years ago. We had A Great Dane up in years who had trouble controlling his bladder. We'd know he had an accident when we came and he didn't greet us. Instead, he was hiding in a closet.<br /><br />To me, must of this hyperbole this is akin to the \\"Humans like rocking chairs because it reminds them of the womb\\" hypothesis I find so amusing. Trying to narrow cause/effect into simple experiments or conclusions leaves so many variables on the table as to render the experiment useless and often wrong.<br /><br />Overall I found the book informative, but it had a lot of holes that were filled with trite reasoning and explanation. If someone had never met a dog, this book would be a bad introduction and it would leave the person completely unprepared for the mind of our beloved canines.

Some people may call it as freedom, but it's an absolute waste of time, and totally irresponsible of the parents if they cannot channelize their children's time in the right direction.<br /><br />At the end of the day, I belong to the Chua's school of thought.  If a child has to be raised a certain way, the parent has to do all the work, even at the cost of being unpopular with the child.  It is only the end that matters, and in the end, the children will surely be proud of their success.<br /><br />It's a fine book, and I think every parent should read this book.

0525478817|5.0|My roommate Kaetlyn sat in awkward dismay on the futon as I sobbed on the old, blue love seat across the room. She had her computer on her lap and she tried to distract herself by scanning Facebook and Twitter. The decaf coffee I had made to trick my mind into staying awake had turned cold, and I stared at the bare-black cover of  The Fault in Our Stars.    Felto walked in the room to me sobbing and asked, \\"Is there something wrong?\\"    \\"I finished the book,\\" I gasped between blowing my nose and wiping my eyes with another tissue. A pile laid there on the floor. I had been crying for at least forty pages.    \\"I see that.\\"    \\"It was sad.\\"    \\"I'm sorry,\\" she said, laughing.  \\"That's not funny at all.\\"    Believe it or not, it is actually a rare occurance for me to cry over a book. I can only remember a few times I've felt so much at an end. Therefore, it is easy to conclude that John Green has accomplished something rare. He has made us fall in love with characters so much that we cannot let them go after they speak their last words. He has created a story that breaks hearts; that makes us accept reality as it is and hate how depraved and wicked the world is.    The main character, Hazel Grace, is an atheist sixteen-year-old girl living with cancer, who falls in love with an agnostic seventeen-year-old boy, Augustus. Augustus is a cancer survivor, and Hazel is expecting death in a few years, maybe. She does not know how much time she has left.    Christians might be (I hope they aren't) turned off by the atheist and agnostic comments in the book, but there is a truth in them. Hazel prays even though she doesn't believe in God, or god. She prays because it seems proper to pray; she struggles with knowing why the universe exists as it does. I feel like that's a common state of adolescence: you're in this unknown place and you're not sure why it exists, and you're beginning to realize that you'll never really know because they are not in your textbook.    (Us Christians may say that they haven't found the right textbook yet, but for most people, The Bible is a book. I don't know if they ever categorize it as fiction; I do know that they don't actually see the truth of the words within it.)    And, Hazel has reasons to be atheist. She has a terminal disease, and has no answer as to why she was the one to develop mutant cells. She has met plenty of other teenagers with cancer, and she has seen how their names just get tacked on to an end of a list when they die. They're the grocery list of prayer. She has no reason to believe that she'll be nothing but a name on a list, and a grenade to anyone she has touched emotionally.    So she falls in love--a passionate, unconditional love. Augustus loves her in the same way. They enter a relationship they know cannot last; they see the danger from the beginning. Unlike most teenage relationships, there are no rosy sunglasses beneath their eyes. And it breaks your heart because their love doesn't end in the book, and you want to fight the universe so that they could live and be together and get in fights and break up like normal teenagers. But nothing is normal for them.    It makes you think about your own life and your own relationships and you realize how fragile it all is. How lucky you are that you know you have a chance to grow old with someone. You realize that you're lucky you have a God that is good to you and who does nothing in vain. But then you realize that you can't comfort yourself in this tragedy. Hazel and Augustus would not find a pleasant afterlife.    I know, I know. They're characters.  That comforts me a lot.I don't actually have to worry about what happens to them after the novel ends.  I still struggled a lot with all of this as I read the TFiOS, but I as I sat, looking at the black cover (I had taken the sleeve off to keep it nice), I realized that I ached to start reading it again as soon as I finished it.    But I wouldn't change a single part of the story. That happens to me. I guess there is a first for everything. It may beat out Northanger Abbey as my favorite.    Well done, John Green, well done. I saw the words &#34;able to kill a man with her bare hands&#34; and moved on.<br /><br />Here's what I loved about the story:<br />*Katsa can kill with her bare hands, but she doesn't like to (I also loved her name).<br />*Without her greedy uncle knowing, King Randa, Katsa forms a council that helps strangers in other lands who are faced with injustices.  She leads the council in investigating distant rumors, forming plans, and carrying out deeds to help others in need when no one else will.<br />*Katsa thinks her Grace is killing, she finds out it's more than that later in the story.<br />*Katsa and Po become instant friends after he learns she has rescued his imprisoned grandfather.  It turns out that Po is also a graceling and he gives Katsa his trust.<br />*Po spends much of the story proving himself to Katsa and respects her.  He watches her without showing it and understands her better than anyone else.<br />*Katsa learns she can control her rage and considers every angle before deciding to fight someone.  She's always considering consequences for herself and others she cares about.<br />*Katsa finally decides to stand up to her greedy uncle and in doing so forfeits the only home she's ever had.<br />*Katsa is a fair fighter and displays mercy even when she is instructed to be brutal.<br />*There are 7 kingdoms and 7 kings in this story-represents a perfect number in God's kingdom.<br />*Katsa doesn't ever want to get married or have children because she sees herself as a killer and is afraid of getting close to anyone.  She also believes that falling in love will cause her to lose her identity and control of her own life.<br /><br />This book is so well written and it pulls the reader in right from the first page.  Katsa's fights and movements are described more as a dance; I saw more beauty in her physical abilities than brutality.  She's also very in tuned to her environment and she's supernaturally strong.  She has no personality and doesn't understand her emotions or how to enjoy herself, but I would guess that anyone in her situation would be the same way.  She doesn't like injustice or bullies and she's very good at perceiving a person's true nature.  It's impossible to sneak up on her, lie to her, mistreat others in front of her, or approach her.  However, she's passionate, genuine, and protective.  I so enjoyed the romance between her and Po, he learns her quickly but allows her to feel comfortable around him.  He lets her lead, but he also teaches her to control her anger and she listens to him.  He's also very perceptive and in tune.<br /><br />There are two love scenes between Katsa and Po and that was the only inappropriate content I came across.  The first one is only a paragraph in length and doesn't give details, but it's descriptive enough and since the characters are in love it will seem justified to some readers (it was disappointing for me).  The second time it happens, it's more implied and the scene moves on.  There is no profanity in the book at all and the most of the book is about the journey Katsa and Po go on together while discovering their feelings for one another as well as the extent of their graces/skills.<br /><br />I didn't finish the book for personal reasons, but it's towards the end they both have to face one king who is the true villain in the story and the plot intensifies at this point.  Even though Katsa's greedy uncle is a manipulating and controlling man, he is not the real villain.  I enjoyed reading about the differences between each king and the how Katsa is determined to do the right thing even though she has been shunned as a graceling. 0439023521|4.0|I was a little skeptical about The Hunger Games. After all, the Twilight series just didn't give me a lot of confidence in our mass culture's ability to select really good books recently. But I eventually didn't like being left out and decided that I might give it a try, and when the film came out, I really didn't want my students to be giving the ending away to me. So, I started it and pretty well read it that night. It was good.<br /><br />I like how Collins has managed to take several adventure-tale plot structures and balance them all together. There's a standard underdog versus the world story, a brewing rebellion versus a tyrannical power plotline, the being hunted on a deserted island type of plot, and even a love triangle throughout. Plus, there are hints--with the mockingjay symbol, for instance--of a mythical side of this story.<br /><br />I admired, too, how Collins managed her characters. Katniss and Peeta are heroic, but they're also far from invulnerable, far from flawed, and far from being superheroes. Additionally, Collins was clever in making the story appropriately intense and violent without either copping out (Peeta and Katniss do participate in the violence) or making the protagonist(s) lose their integrity and likeability.<br /><br />The novel did leave me with several questions. I keep wondering, for instance, how to reconcile the very high level of technology that Panem clearly has with both the seemingly low population and the level of anxiety they show toward the colonies. Maybe those things will be clarified in later books, but for now, they leave me wondering.<br /><br />I am also wondering, though, in the way that every writer wants their readers to wonder...I am wondering what is going to happen next. I have already purchased the next couple of books in the series, but I am waiting until I'm going to have enough time to devote to them. I know that once I start, I'm pretty well going to put the rest of my life on hold until I'm through.

B006PZBR8S|4.0|This is essentially a father-son bonding experience over a boxing robot. Hugh plays an absentee father and former boxer who makes a living controlling a robot against various adversaries. Dakota is soon thrust in his lap, finds an aptitude for the sport, and becomes Hugh's better half in the enterprise as they travel around boxing. Dakota eventually obtains his own robot, and the two field it against the top robot champion in a Rocky-esque showdown.<br /><br />It's a very slickly-produced movie. The special effects are flawless: we might as well inhabit a world with giant lumbering robots. The fight scenes are least as exciting as those in Warrior and better than anything in Transformers. The latter is particularly surprising; the robots here are the equivalent of RC cars, but through evocative design and the emotional investment of the human characters, they have more personality than the sentient Transformers.<br /><br />There is a romantic subplot that involves the daughter of Hugh's former trainer, but it's of no consequence. More interesting are the various unscrupulous characters that inhabit Hugh's underworld early in the  movie. The real standout, though, is Dakota. He's absolutely convincing as hurt, slighted son, and later in his bonding with Hugh and the robot. Tremendous range in general, and not just for a child actor. He constantly steals scenes from Hugh.<br /><br />I do have a few nits to pick. This version of Hugh is significantly dumber than Wolverine. His early misfortunes are self-inflicted from errors improbable for the experienced robot handler he portrays. It's hard to suspend disbelief when your hard-luck protagonist blows his last shot by half-assing fight preparation. There's also a lack of personal growth at odds with the upbeat ending. He doesn't care about the kid at the outset, but while he becomes less bitter as the movie progresses, he ends up less a father than an accomplice. A number of like plot threads are left open after the final match. This is all a bit unsatisfying, but leaves the franchise primed for a sequel that should appear in 2014.<br /><br />My second nit involves some rather heavy-handed string-pulling by the director. We've got shots of the cute kid with a single tear, longing gazes by the romantic interest, extended montages, some truly cheeseball announcer commentary, and an emotional score that announces itself when we're supposed to feel something. The pity of it is, the actors don't need all this assistance, and most of it just pulled me out of the story.  The pastiche of Hollywoodisms isn't so egregious as in something like Pearl Harbor, though it does take away from could otherwise have been a fairly powerful film.<br /><br />All that aside, it's a very original premise with lots of excitement and good acting. The plot arc was transparent almost from the get-go, but I still wanted to stick around to see how it played out. That's a pretty solid endorsement.<br /><br />And how is it for children? The CGI robot battles won't raise eyebrows, though the two lead humans do get beaten up in one short scene. Obscenities are rare. The F-bomb doesn't make an appearance. There's no onscreen sex or anything resembling it. Gambling is a constant presence. The emotional scenes are no more traumatic than those in the Land Before Time. I'd say that any kid 8 or above should be fine.it unsatisfying, but leaves the franchise primed for a sequel that should appear in 2014. B005MF1O5S|5.0|My children became firm fans of Doctor Who some years ago and consequently periodically I have the pleasure of watching the stories with them as we all sit on the sofa together. Quite a while ago I read an article in which one of the writers - perhaps it was Stephen Moffat, though it was back in the days when RTD was the script wrangler for the series - that a really good Doctor Who episode is the equivalent to a 60-minute film. It should be more or less complete in and of itself, and so packed with ideas, great dialog and excellent camerawork that you're left feeling totally satisfied. Every once in a while Doctor Who achieves this gold standard. For me, such perfect episodes include Rose, The Girl In the Fireplace, Blink, Turn Left, the very first episode with Matt Smith as the Eleventh Doctor, and now this one.<br /><br />Not only are we carried along a dual-time stream plot (one compressed a la Othello, the other linear as we watch) that is full of pathos, but in addition the camerawork is absolutely superb. This may be the single best and most consistent use of camera-as-storyteller I've ever seen. Much of the effect is achieved by counterpointing the aged Amy with her younger self in very clever side-by-side shots and overlays. It's really easy to do this kind of thing badly so the viewer is aware of the trick at the expense of the emotion; here it's the opposite, with the camerawork actually pushing the emotion deeper and further than it would otherwise go with straight shots. This is the kind of direction and camerawork that deserves an award - yet it's easy not to notice it particularly because it's all in the service of the story, which is compelling, sad, and doesn't provide an easy way out when the ultimate choice must be made: which Amy to save?<br /><br />For me, one of the very satisfying things was the clever set-up for the denoeument: the hand-bots phrase \\"this is a kindness.\\" At first, because the \\"kindness\\" will in fact be lethal to Amy, it's just scary - a great contradiction between word and deed. But in the end, when the Tardis must depart and leave the aged Amy behind, the words come true. It is, at the very end, in a heartbreaking kind of way, after all a kindness.<br /><br />If you don't know much about Doctor Who, this episode is as good as any as a place to start - and then you can have the pleasure of going all the way back to the first episode of the reboot with Christopher Ecclestone as the Ninth Doctor, and go forward from there. Or, go in any direction you want, in a wibbly-wobbly timey-wimey sort of way. Any route at all will be great fun and very satisfying.scary - a great contradiction between word and deed. This is what happened to Christine Lucas, now a middle-aged woman living outside London with her husband, Ben.  Every day he must remind her who he is and that they have been married for a long time.  He leaves instructions for household chores to keep her busy and occupied during the day.<br /><br />In order to get some greatly needed help, Christine secretly begins to see a doctor, and he suggests writing down each day in a journal everything that she remembers. She can then go back and read what her memory has told her the day before, thereby rebuilding, in part, some of her former life. That's the reality of being human. We can't always control ourselves according to 'civilized' society. Travis's constantly flaring temper is huge part of what makes him who he is, what causes him to be the 'screw up' he believes himself to be, and it's up to Abby to love him anyway. That's what this book is about - loving someone for who they are, not trying to change or save them, but loving them without conditions.<br /><br />I wish I could say this book was an easy and light read - it's anything but that. It's an intense and extremely emotional ride from start to finish. 1400069289|4.0|Duhigg's book picks apart how habits work (both to help us and hurt us) from multiple angles, illuminatingly and entertainingly. This book looks at habits of individuals, organizations, and whole societies, examining how we form them and how we can change them. Topics include making an exercise habit, breaking a drinking habit, creating a civil rights movement and rescuing a major manufacturing firm from stagnation.<br /><br />In one chapter, he examines how entire companies can form structures of authority and cooperation that either propel them to success or drive them to utter disaster. In another, he describes how small \\"keystone habits\\" can entirely remake a person's life with surprisingly little further effort. A person who starts a regular exercise routine will often get their finances and career in order almost automatically. Alcoa (a major aluminum manufacturer) emerged from a long downward spiral starting simply with an intense focus on employee safety.<br /><br />Duhigg examines the latest neuroscience of habit formation as well as A.A., one of the most successful habit-changing groups in recent history. He looks at what it means to form a habit in the first place, how a habit can be changed (replaced, rather than extinguished, to be precise), and how corporations like Target are seeking to influence your habits.<br /><br />This book covers a lot of ground, and the variety of material he uses to explain these concepts keeps the book deeply involving the whole way through. If you're interested in pscyhology and why we do what we do every day, this is definitely a book for you.

It certainly thunderously redeems the sub-genre of the found footage film. In that lost trove of found footage film, the [REC] franchise and, more recently, CHRONICLE, set themselves apart. I'd even add CLOVERFIELD to that short list, except I don't want my feelings hurt.<br /><br />Paella, Pau Gasol, and [REC] - this is Spain hooking up the rest of the world. See, when done right, the found film footage provides chills that feel more visceral and immediate. [REC] begins in humdrum manner, with a television crew deployed to a local fire station in Barcelona to do a piece on firefighters or, as the Spanish are wont to call them, \\"los bomberos\\" (which sounds cooler). When the firefighters on shift respond to an emergency call, the television crew accompanies them to an apartment tenement in which an elderly woman is screaming non-stop. But a routine call soon spirals out of control when the health authorities arrive to seal off the building, stranding the news team, the firefighters, policemen, and the residents with an undefined menace. But I'll define it... and SPOILER it.... old Mrs. Izquierdo is a zombie.<br /><br />Here's a neat thing: Director of Photography, Pablo Rosso, not only wields the point-of-view camera himself but he also played the role of the in-movie news team's cameraman \\"Pablo.\\" I'll tell you what, it takes some sort of steely fiber for someone to keep hoisting that camera while spooky paranormal shizzy is going down.<br /><br />The plot isn't deep. The narrative is populated by stock characters. That's okay. [REC] overcomes the shackles of its tropes by dint of its vitality and nervy execution and presentation. It's rated R for \\"bloody horror violence and language,\\" but it also should've mentioned the film's knack for inducing nerve-jangling claustrophobia. The building which traps our characters probably looks welcoming and quaint by daylight, but at night and as seen thru the confines of a handheld camera, the environs transmogrify into these disquieting dark and cramped quarters. This frightener takes full advantage of its conceit, of this one camera providing the first person perspective. The most alarming concern becomes not about what's in front of us, but rather the thing that could be waiting to pounce on us just off-camera. That there's no soundtrack at all until the end credits roll bolsters that sense of jagged, stripped-down believability. The third act is relentless in its visceral scares. B007J4T2G8|4.0|Multidimensional and thought-provoking, Fifty Shades of Grey is a story that will twist you, bend you and cry for you to submit to its intrigue.<br /><br />Okay, normally I am not one to grab a book that is being \\"hyped up\\" in the media.  However, when Fifty Shades of Grey began to pop up everywhere, the nerd in me came bursting forth and Googled the title.  Yes, I Googled it.  What I found was a very varying and diverse thought spectrum - which is actually a good thing, in my opinion.  It seems that people either loved it or hated it.  Now, what really grabbed me was an article that I read where some psychologist or Dr. of some sort was saying how this book was considered \\"Mommy Porn\\" and that it was a very unhealthy thing for woman.  Um, excuse me?!  It is socially acceptable for men to look at and ogle women/porn online, in bars, in magazines, etc, but if a woman reads something that is even slightly touted as \\"erotic\\" it is unhealthy?!  Let's see - a woman reads and thereby uses her intelligence to  take in the words and has to use her creativity and imagination to bring these images, portrayed within the story, to life for herself and that is unhealthy?  How is this any worse than a person reading murder mysteries, horror stories and anything of violence?  Needless to say, this ticked me off.  I knew right then and there that I had to get the book and read it myself.  Now, to take a step back, people may say \\"How can it be acceptable to allow a man to tie you up, make you eat when he wants, etc?\\"  Well, it is not.  However, if there are two consenting adults and they get their kicks that way - well, let them go forth and dom/sub.  I do want to say, however, that these issues were blown waaaay out of proportion with this book.  There is no where near the type of dominance that some say is so horrible and demeaning to women (and for the record, there are books out there were the men are the submissive - it is fictional entertainment after all) within Fifty Shades of Grey.  Okay, on with the review of the actual book.<br /><br />For me, Fifty Shades of Grey was a great look into the psyche of a man who grew up with a tortured start to life.  He learns to cope in life with strong walls and a hard shell built around himself.  He does not allow himself to become close to any one person and relationships with woman are something to enjoy physically and devoid of emotions.  So far within his life, he has been able to keep things going at this level.  That all changes, however, when he meets Anastasia (Ana).  For Christian, Ana is unlike any other woman he has ever met before.  She is not only beautiful and intelligence, she is innocence and strength.  Ana does not bend over backwards for Christian and she knows her own mind and morals.  I want to mention that this is something that pleasantly surprised me.  When I first read about this book, I had pictured Ana as a very submissive person who would be, more or less, a puppet on a string.  I was not thrilled with this idea, however I pushed it aside as I began to read.  Ana is not at all like my preconceived notions.  Nor, for that matter, was Christian the jerk, dominant \\"I am man, do as I say\\" type of character that I had thought he would be.<br /><br />I fully believe that the reader will get out of Fifty Shades of Grey what they put into it.  I have heard, repeatedly, about the hot sex, the bondage, etc of this book.  Yes, there is sex.  Yes it is steamier than Harlequin, however it is not nearly as sexually explicit as some erotica books out on the market.  There is one scene of slight violence, however it was handled in a way that truly lent to the book.  I am not going to go into this any further because I do not want to give anything away.  As far as bondage?  Slight.  Being controlling?  Slight.  An actual story of love?  Yes.  A story that goes deeper than sex?  Absolutely yes.<br /><br />Going in, I really had no expectation for Fifty Shades of Grey.  I was neutral and curious.  This is what I took from it:<br /><br />Christian is an amazing character.  He has some major issues that began at a very early age, which have damaged him deeply.  He has a need for control on a psychological level that goes beyond machismo.  He doesn't feel the need for control for a shallow reason, but one to survive.  He is a lost soul who needs to find salvation.<br /><br />Ana is a young innocent college grad.  She has the world in front of her.  Ana has also never felt the need or desire for a physical relationship with a man.  That all changes, however, when she inadvertently meets Christian.  Her world spins in a way she worries she will lose control of, but manages not to drown in.<br /><br />Together, Christian and Ana battle emotional demons as the sparks of heady chemistry roars between them.  For Christian, Ana presents many \\"firsts\\", which both surprise and scare him.  For reasons beyond him, Ana is different and reaches into him unlike anyone he has ever met before.  Ana battles her sub-conscience and \\"inner goddess\\" to figure out what is right for her and how far she is willing to let herself stretch for Christian.<br /><br />I truly adored both of these characters.  They came to life in an amazing way for me and truly made me care for both of them - particularly Christian.  A bit of food for thought:  Christian asks that Ana's hands (or whomever he is having a relationship with) to be bound during sex.  His biggest fear?  To have his chest touched.  Connection?  See, for me, the bondage issue went way beyond kinky sex.<br /><br />In any case, I really enjoyed Fifty Shades of Grey.  I can't wait to continue with the trilogy to delve deeper into Christian's past and discover the demons that have tortuously branded him.  I anxiously look forward to seeing how the lives of both Ana and Christian unfold.  For me, this was a story of two lives who deeply connect and love one another.  One of those people unable to form a healthy and \\"normal\\" relationship, nor ever had the desire to, until now.  This was a story of strength, demons, trust and faith.  It was not a story of sex.<br /><br />I do want to mention that the story was not perfect for me.  The writing was very good, however many times the author would pop in words that seemed out of place within the story.  The words would be the type of words not normally used in every day conversation (at least for me) and would break up the flow of the story for me a bit.  Some examples?  Infinitesimally, envisages, medulla oblongata (ex: And from a very tiny, underused part of my brain - probably located at the base of my medulla oblongata where my subconscious dwells - comes the thought:), and there are others throughout, these are just a few that stood out.  E.L. James does use amazing word visualization and easily drew me into the story and the scenes.  When the scenes were sizzling, they were hot, when the scenes were emotionally tortured, they were very heart-tugging and when the scenes were sweet, they were heart-melting.  All-in-all, Fifty Shades of Grey is a wonderful book and one that I highly recommend - not because it is so media \\"hyped\\" but because it is a wonderful story - just please do not read it simply for the sex, but delve into the complex emotional and psychological aspects of the story.  And for the record - I have heard this being dubbed the \\"dirty Twilight\\" books.  Honestly?  Other than a tortured male character and an innocent female character - there is absolutely no similarities - at least none that I could find.urious. B002ZB26AO|5.0|I've been trying to learn all that I can about North Korea in order to educate myself and my children. I will never pretend to comprehend or truly understand what life is like there; but I believe Demick accomplished what she set out to do: to tell the stories of everyday life for North Korean defectors, particularly during the 1990's famine, how they survived, in what conditions, and their subsequent escape. They are stories of survival and above all else, hope. There is young love, old love, lost love, pain, suffering, joy and sadness. The will to live and the ability to withstand so much hardship and sorrow is beyond anything I can fathom. I simply cannot wrap my head around the courage it would take to risk everything you've ever known and escape to a world you know nothing about; all the while knowing at any minute you could be captured and sent to a labor camp for who know's how long, or worse, executed. Sadly, most stories of North Korean defector's don't end \\"happily ever after,\\" as we all like to assume. Most struggle to adjust; the guilt, shame, lonliness, worry for family left behind, all can sabotage any happiness or contentment they had hoped to find. Chol-hwan Kang, a North Korean defector, labor camp survivor and author of his memoir, \\"The Aquariums of Pyongyang\\", said in an interview: \\"Many people don't understand North Korea and the mentality of the defectors.\\" It's just hard to understand how this is still going on in the 21st Century!

The first book doesn't answer all of these questions - so of course the minute I finished, I beat a path to my nearest bookstore and bought the other 2 books in the series.  If you wanta great escape - I highly recommend this series.

Jun Do never knew his mother - we are told that she is a singer of great beauty who was shipped to Pyongyang. His name, like those of the other orphans, is given to him from the list of the 114 Grand Martyrs of the Revolution. He is, simultaneously, everyone and no-one. Jun Do even sounds like John Doe.<br /><br />After the orphanage is devastated, Jun Do is sent to the military where first he undertakes training in zero-light combat in the tunnels under the demilitarized zone, and then on an undercover mission which involves kidnapping Japanese from the beaches. And then, Jun Do is sent to language school to learn English, which gets him assigned to a boat to transcribe radio intercepts. Once back on land, he is assigned to an intelligence team travelling to Texas where he meets a Senator and his wife.<br /><br />`There's no way around it: to get a new life, you've got to trade in your old one.'<br /><br />After returning from Texas, Jun Do ends up in a labour camp where he takes over the life and identity of a North Korean military hero, Commander Ga. In this half of the novel, the depiction of North Korea may exceed a reader's wildest imaginings. `The Dear Leader' Kim Jong-il, who died shortly before this book was published, is Commander Ga's rival for the affections of Commander Ga's wife, an actress named Sun Moon.<br /><br />It's complicated, and convoluted and doesn't always make sense. It's jarring at times to switch from the omniscient narrator who tells the `real' story, to the ever-present loudspeakers that tell the version of the story the government wants its citizens to hear and then, in the second half of the novel, to include the interrogator whose responsibility is to make sure that every citizen's story is told. In the end, after most of the pieces fall into place, Pak Jun Do does really become a hero - but not an official North Korean one. I'm ambivalent about setting a dystopian novel in a country where fiction can easily be read as fact but, in the end, it's that blurring of possibility that makes this novel such a powerful read. B006PTL1QM|3.0|by Ilene Serlin, Ph.D.<br /><br />Film Review: A Dangerous Method--A Woman's Perspective<br />Published on January 24, 2012 by Ilene A. Serlin , Ph.D. in Make Your Life a Blessing<br />There are many ways to review this film, both professional and personal. Let me start with the personal first, since I have strong feelings about it.<br /><br />For many years, I have been intrigued by Sabina Spielrein, a woman pioneer of psychoanalysis. My own life, though nothing like hers, has led me into adventures with exotic locations, studies, or high-power, charismatic men. Consequently, I have maintained an informal and formal fascination with women like Sabina Spielrein and questions about their lives; I attended a seminar on her at the Jung Institute, read A Secret Symmetry: Sabina Spielrein Between Freud and Jung, and A Most Dangerous Method, one of her articles, designed and taught a course on Women and Narrative, and published articles: \\"The Anne Sexton Complex\\", \\"A Tribute to Laura Perls\\", \\"Dancing Women's Freedom: the Story of Isadora Duncan\\", and tributes to other women in psychology and the arts. I am a Fellow of the Society for Women in Psychology (Div. 35) of the American Psychological Association. The women in my reading list included biographies of women who were muses to famous men (Rodin, Picasso), wives of famous men (the Beat poets, Scott Fitzgerald). Most of these women either self-destructed (Marilyn Monroe) or were hospitalized by their husbands (T.S. Eliot). The women who survived were writers who advocated having a \\"room of one's own\\" and an \\"independent income\\" (Virginia Woolf, Vita Sackville-West), left a man's world (Carolyn Heilbrun left Columbia), capitalized on their experiences (Francoise Gilot), or were lesbian or bisexual (Diane diPrima). In other words, sex between men and women can be a powerful drug-profound, uplifting, creative and destructive.<br /><br />Next, since I did my pre-doctoral internship at the C. G. Jung Institute and taught atthe institute in Zurich, I have been also fascinated by the personality and legends around Carl Jung. Being involved and interested in cases of erotic countertransference (as an analysand and a supervisor), I read Jungian analyst Robert Stein's Incest and Human Love, and consulted with Mario Jacoby; in short, the boundary issues among Jungian psychoanalysts were (in)famous and fairly widespread, often with elaborate rationalizations for why boundary crossing was permissible. I also heard more stories about how both male and female lay analysts were trained by being in analysis with Jung (or Freud) and one of the inner circle; incest was part of the excitement. I found the book, Jung's Circle of Women: The Valkyries. Finally, I met with Aniela Jaffe, Jung's personal secretary whom, legend had it, was one of the few heterosexual women who had not slept with Jung.<br /><br />Aniela Jaffe, blind and almost 90 years old at the time, took my hand, pronounced it strong, and advised me to \\"have the courage to lead an unconventional life.\\" She told me that she tried marriage for 6 years, found it not for her, and preferred to live like a graduate student in a simple, book-lined apartment in Zurich. I identified with her-she, a well-educated Jewish upper middle-class woman from Berlin; me, a well-educated Jewish woman from New York (Russian heritage), Sabina Spielrein, a well-educated Jewish Russian woman living in Zurich and Vienna, and Toni Wolff, Jung's next long-time mistress, a Jewish or half-Jewish former patient and originator of some of his theories of the occult.<br /><br />An unconventional life or marriage? This is one question that has haunted me for a long time. My role models, including Simone de Beauvoir, said that it made women property and advocated personal and professional liberation. Modern women ask these questions, some of which I reviewed for PsycCritiques covering Elizabeth Gilbert's Committed: A Skeptic Makes Peace with Marriage.<br /><br />In her real life, Sabina Spielrein, as she got mentally stronger, thought that she should have her own man and family. She married a fellow Russian Jewish medical student, had two children, but was reportedly soon bored. She left Vienna and moved back to Rostov-on-Don with her family. Although she accomplished much--she was responsible for the contact and collaboration between Jung and Freud and did try to keep them together, she originated theories about the death instinct and wrote about 30 articles, she trained the early generation of psychoanalysts in Russia-her life was cut short in 1941 when she was killed by the Nazis.<br /><br />Would it have been better to remain in Vienna amidst the creative ferment? Would she have kept productive and did she need the madness/edginess/competition to create? Is love hotter when it is forbidden and secret? As Spielrein wrote in her own diary (February 22, 1912):<br /><br /> No ashes, no coal can burn with such glow as a secretive love of which no one must know.<br /><br />Kay Jamison, herself a sufferer of bipolar disorder, tackled this question in Touched with Fire: Manic-Depressive Illness and the Artistic Temperament. In her earlier writings, she came down on the side of sobriety; creativity is not helped by manic-depressive disorders and medication is in order. In her later writings, she did acknowledge the contribution of manic-depressive illness to creativity. Anne Sexton also tried to have a normal 1950s suburban married life to keep herself grounded, became bored and left--but committed suicide anyway. So did Sylvia Plath.<br /><br />What is a talented woman to do?<br /><br />One could say that in Victorian times, women suffered from repression, and showed classical symptoms of hysteria. However, the treatment of choice for them might have been an independent income and a job; Freud's Anna O., who walked out of his office, went on to become a well-known social worker. Ellen West, who was expertly diagnosed and phenomenologically described by Binswanger, died anyway; but Kim Chernin, a feminist psychologist, re-analyzed the case by prescribing a job and independence for Ellen West.<br /><br />These are some of the questions raised for me once again by seeing the film. I don't think there are easy answers, and certainly we are no longer in Victorian times; the issues were particular to those times, but also universal and applicable to women today.<br /><br />Some quick comments about the film itself: I thought it was just OK. First, Jung was a big, hearty charismatic man; in the film, he was rather sensitive and effete. Second, Freud was powerful and deep; in the film, I thought he was just dark and somewhat creepy. Third, Sabina Spielrein was a strong woman; in the film, Kira Knightley over-acted her symptoms and desperation. Emma Jung was large and strong, not the delicate beauty in the film. None of the characters in the film, although they made appropriate gestures and conversation, touched me. Finally, the explicit sexual scenes left me puzzled. I thought that it was never confirmed whether their relationship was \\"consummated\\" and wondered how such liberties could be taken.<br /><br />In short, this film is psychologically rich, intriguing, and leaves the viewer with more questions than answers.<br /><br />Bio: Ilene Serlin is a Fellow of the American Psychological Association and the Society for the Study of Women (Div. 35). She has taught \\"Women and Narrative\\" and written about psychology and women for over 35 years.<br /><br />References:<br />Berger, M.R., Serlin, I.A., & Siderits, M.A. (2007) Dancing women's freedom: The story of Isadora Duncan. In Women of Vision: Their psychology, circumstances, and success. E. Gavin, A. Clamar, M.A. Siderits (Eds.). Springer Publishing. 11-29.<br /><br />Serlin, I. A. & Criswell, E. (2001). Humanistic psychology and women: A critical-historical perspective (Ed. K. Schneider, J, Bugental, J. Pierson). Handbook of Humanistic Psychology: Leading Edges of Theory, Research, and Practice. Thousand Oaks, CA: Sage Publications. 29-36.<br /><br />Serlin, I. A., (1994). The Anne Sexton complex. In May, R. & Schneider, K. (Ed.), The psychology of existence: an integrative clinical perspective. New York: Harper & Row.<br /><br />Serlin, I. A., Classen, C., Frances, B. & Angell, K. (2000). Symposium: Support groups for women with breast cancer. The Arts in Psychotherapy. Vol. 27, No. 2, 123-138.<br /><br />Serlin, I. A., (1998). Interview with Anna Halprin. Somatics, Fall/Winter, 14-20. Reprint of American Journal of Dance Therapy (1996).<br /><br />Serlin, I. (1992). On meeting a remarkable woman. Association for Transpersonal Psychology Newsletter.<br /><br />Serlin, I. (1992). Tribute to Laura Perls. Journal of Humanistic Psychology, 32, No. 3, 57-66.<br /><br />Serlin, I. (1990). Exploitation in therapy. Association of Humanistic Psychology Perspective. June 10.
