B004HEXSLI|5.0|Many nueroanatomists try to make sense of the human brain and how it functions with mental behavior. Not many people have the opportunity to experience the realities of their brain and academic knowledge outside of the laboratory, unless they undergo a complex medical condition. It's always a different experience when someone with expertise is placed in that position- or when their body forces them in that situation.<br />Jill Bolte Taylor, Ph.D. was actually put in that situation and was able to do more than just suffer from the experience. She had the ability to learn from the stroke and to communicate her learning to an interested audience. The book, My Stroke of Insight, is an extraordinary memoir written by Dr. Taylor. Dr. Taylor is a neuroanatomist who developed her knowledge as a brain scientist through a Ph.D. at Indiana University and post-graduate fellowship at Harvard University. She was elected to serve on the Board of Directors for NAMI, the National Alliance on Mental Illness, advocating the \\"tissue issue,\\" asking people to donate their brains to science. Interestingly enough, she was able to apply all her knowledge when she experienced a stroke in her left hemisphere at the age of 37. Eight years later Dr. Taylor completely recovered from this severe brain hemorrhage and wrote a memoir to share her experience.<br />One of the main points that Dr. Taylor wants her readers to understand is that both brain hemispheres are able to adapt to change and recover from injuries. She learned that, as one hemisphere fails, the other hemisphere still has the capability to allow a person to function. Her remaining right hemisphere was filled with gratitude for life, focusing on current moments and allowing her to break the boundaries of stereotypes, race, and religion. Conversely, Dr. Taylor's damaged, but previously dominant, left hemisphere remained preoccupied with forms of communication, details, and routine. Dr. Taylor found a new gift of identifying patterns, memorizing, and even allowing herself to consciously improve her left mind's ego center as well.<br />She also stresses being able to identify the signs of a stroke to get help sooner and therefore possibly minimize stroke damage. Dr. Taylor explains how the acronym \\"STROKE\\" summarizes the signs of a stroke: S=Speech that is impaired, T=Tingling or Numbness, R= Remembering difficulties, O= Off balance with coordination, K= Killer Headache, E= Eye problems.<br />Dr. Taylor covers multiple topics, but divides the book into 20 different chapters, such as \\"Jill's Pre-Stroke Life,\\" \\"Simple Science,\\" and \\"Day Two: The Morning After.\\" The basic brain terminology for the reader's understanding is essential, but an expert runs the risk that the average reader may roll his or her eyes and put the book down. Dr. Taylor decided to present science in chapters two and three, but keeps it simple through small pictures that help readers understand the anatomy of her cognitive, physical, and spiritual experiences. She presents images and explains the functions of the gyrus and the sulcus, defines neurotransmitters, traces our brain back to our ancestors, and observes brain plasticity. She then gives a personal account of the morning of the stroke, what it was like to be without a language or the capability to understand numbers, and how she began a new life again with the help of her mom, G.G.<br />The book is an inspirational memoir reminding the reader that growth and healing are possible even under the worst circumstances. Dr. Taylor describes her recovery as stages of normal human development by learning how to walk, talk, and read again. What makes this memoir inspiring is the effort she dedicates to her brain. She emphasizes trying difficult and unfamiliar tasks because if she never made this effort, her recovery might not have happened. What makes it even more inspiring is the fact that during the whole process, she kept repeating to herself that it could be worse. If she could not remember something, she and G.G. made a point to make a new memory. Imaging the patience it must have took to be taught infant steps in an adult body and still having enough hope is inspiring. I am filled with respect for this woman!<br />Dr. Taylor must have done something right to recover from this stroke. According to Dr. Taylor, in her chapter \\"What I Needed the Most,\\" her recovery was successful because people treated her as though she was actually going to recover by supporting and encouraging her. Her recovery process was effective in part because G.G. allowed her to get plenty of sleep, unlike most rehabilitation centers that make the patient work around the center's rigid schedule. G.G.'s presence also allowed for a personalized schedule of cognitive exercises. When Dr. Taylor felt well rested after about six hours, she was willing to put in about 20 minutes for more strenuous tasks to avoid headaches and to focus her attention. Interestingly, she honored her brain when it reached a maximum level of input, and then focused on more rest. She took this sleep seriously: lights out, TV off, and earplugs in to ensure sleep.<br />Most importantly, the main connection Dr. Taylor makes is the plasticity she experiences in her brain. Plasticity explains why her writing is simple and how she becomes adventurous, empathetic, and full of new emotions. With a left hemisphere that remained damaged nonetheless, the complex functionality of her brain allowed her to read, speak, and learn again. Dr. Taylor had to explore the world's textures, features, and characteristics as if it was for the first time. With assistance from G.G. she learned what it was like to walk on sidewalks, that the lines in the cement had no meaning, and what it was like to slide her foot on ice. She even adjusted to the radiant light in a grocery store by wearing sunglasses.<br />Overall, I believe this book can be a great read for a wide range of audiences. The small anatomical images that help illustrate simple science in chapters two and three are perfect for most students. It is also beneficial to adults who are not as familiar with the brain and what a stroke is. For those who are out of their zone of expertise or perhaps are interested in gaining knowledge about what a stroke is, this book offers assessment questions focusing on symptoms that may be present before a stroke. Dr. Taylor explains some of the symptoms and what to look for in this kind of situation. The book also offers detailed explanations of the differences between a blood clot, aneurysm, and normal blood flow stated in simple terms. Lastly, in my opinion, this book would be most beneficial to stroke survivors and their caregivers. It reveals an unknown path of evidence to what most stroke patients cannot tell us. Dr. Taylor's list of forty simple things she needed most after her stroke is great evidence to what most caregivers or readers are looking for.<br />In conclusion, if you're interested in a simple read, I would recommend My Stroke of Insight. If the catchy chapter titles previously stated don't already intrigue you, she makes it easy to understand important topics by bolding sentences. She writes with a tone of gratitude for life and, most importantly, uses simple language, which gives the reader a sense of appreciation for her. As a prestigious neuroanatomist, she makes the effort to make the book comprehensible, instead of trying to present herself as a significant and formal scientist. She stresses that her brain disorder did not destroy her life and through others she was able to put her life back together. All she asks of you is to exchange this information with loved ones.uld not remember something, she and G.G. made a point to make a new memory. Imaging the patience it must have took to be taught infant steps in an adult body and still having enough hope is inspiring. B0056C6XIA|4.0|First, I have to say that all the tools on this item do what they are made for very well. The scissors, knives, sawblade, etc. work exceptionally well, and don't disappoint me. And the best part: the flashlight is extremely bright. I haven't needed to use the fire striker yet, though. The only thing that bugs me is the quality. The first time you hold it in your hand, it feels like it's going to fall apart. Opening the pliers isn't smooth. Instead, opening it kind of feels like the hinges are oiled with molasses. The blades, although excellent at doing what they do, are made with a very plastic-felling lightweight metal. If you get this multi-tool, you should put some WD-40, or some kind of lubricant on the metal pieces, I think it will help. Finally, opening and closing the various tools requires specialized training of some sort. 0399159029|4.0|Having followed the Bernie Gunther series from it's inception, I was ambivalent about this, the most recent installment.  The first 3 books (grouped and sold in a single volume) were simply superior: cunningly crafted, elegantly written, peppered with wry wit, freely bestowed with authorial liberties which admixed historical and fictitious personages and endowed with byzantine (but credible) plots.  After that, the books became cliche and, by the time of \\"Field Gray\\", it seemed the series should be retired.  So, I awaited \\"Prague Fatale\\" with some trepidation.  As it happens, Kerr has pulled Bernie back from the precipice.  Although a bit shopworn, the book has revivified Bernie.<br /><br />In brief, abandoning the chronology of the initial books (Bernie from pre-war inception to post-war malaise) Kerr re-installs the dour, slightly dilapidated, world-weary and terminally cynical detective in early wartime Germany and then into the \\"Protectorate of Bohemia and Moravia\\", the former Czechoslovakia.  Early on in Berlin, Bernie chances upon something that seems what it is not and, by the usual improbable chain of interconnected circumstances, ends up in Prague working for SS Prince of the Realm Heydrich.  Following the (acknowledged) lead of Agatha Christie (the justly famed \\"closed-room\\" murder in \\"Roger Ackroyd\\"), an SS aide de camp is murdered.  A cross section of SS luminaries, one of whom is suspected in the case and also suspected as a spy for the Czech resistance are in residence at the secluded country manor (a psychotic's \\"Downton Abby\\")...who done it?<br /><br />As before, all the SS higher-ups are improbably urbane, eloquent, vaguely resentful of their colleagues, cynically pragmatic, ideologically removed from Nazi claptrap and engaged in barely concealed double-dealings and sordid affairs of one sort or another.  The sophisticated and slightly detached commentary even extends to SS lowlives such as the expert torturer, Sgt. Soppa.  Leaving aside the invented dialogue which is more akin to early post-war Hollywood caricatures of Nazi officers (cultural affectations, sartorial excellence, keen and probing intelligence), Kerr has done immense research into the period and it shows.<br /><br />While having a certain degree of intelligence; a great deal of cunning; an pragmatic inclination; and a lust for power, it seems incredible that Heydrich would indulge Bernie's provocations and challenging insults to the extent suggested herein.  Based on recent comprehensive histories of Himmler (e.g. Longerich's book) and Heydrich (\\"Hitler's Hangman\\"), it seems fantastic that Bernie's head would have rested so smugly, insolently and satisfyingly situated on his neck in real life.  Its also encouraging that Bernie's moral equivocation regarding the fate of the Jews and other unfortunates at the hands of his Nazi colleagues has finally and firmly been secured on the side of the oppressed.  Kerr compensates to some extent by extending a sense of moral ambiguity on the \\"actions\\" in the \\"East\\" to many Nazi satraps:  unfortunately, there is little or nothing in the actual historical literature to support that move.  Finally, and to give away only a minor element of the plot, the postulated reason for Heydrich's (fortunate) turn for the worse and eventual demise is pure baloney: Himmler and Heydrich (according to the authoritative Longerich biography) were ideological intimates and close allies.<br /><br />So, \\"Praque Fatale\\" is certainly entertaining.  Its not quite to the standard of the first 3 books, especially when the absence of witty repart&eacute; and Chandler-like metaphors are factored into consideration.  But its a vast improvement on the 3 immediate predecessors.  Its not a good introduction to the series but its not a bad one, either.  In short, its an encouraging development for Bernie Gunther fans and good enough to encourage the purchase of the next case.d dialogue which is more akin to early post-war Hollywood caricatures of Nazi officers (cultural affectations, sartorial excellence, keen and probing intelligence), Kerr has done immense research into the period and it shows.<br /><br />While having a certain degree of intelligence; a great deal of cunning; an pragmatic inclination; and a lust for power, it seems incredible that Heydrich would indulge Bernie's provocations and challenging insults to the extent suggested herein.
